33:1001:86743:308:7:71:540:372:6:g_d0_f31:6:g_d0_f6:Notes.The figure shows how the unconditional quantile partial effect (UQPE) is approximately estimated for a treatment such as a small increase
33:1002:86886:7:7:72:540:365:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:in the minimum wage.FA(y)represents the actual distribution of outcomey, whileFB(y)is the counterfactual distribution with a higher level
33:1003:87023:7:8:72:540:358:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:of treatment. Under the assumption of conditional independence, the counterfactual distribution can be estimated using distribution regressions
33:1004:87166:8:7:72:539:350:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:of the impact of the policy on the share below cutoffscfor all cutoffs. The UQPE for theτthquantile isQB,τ−QA,τ, represented as the solid
33:1005:87303:7:7:71:539:343:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:(blue) segment. The recentered influence function (RIF) regression approximates the UQPE by inverting the counterfactual CDFFB(y)using a
33:1006:87439:7:10:72:540:336:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:local linear approximation. After defining a cutoffcsuch thatFA(c) =τusing the actual distributionFA(y), it uses the impact on the proportion
33:1007:87580:10:10:71:540:326:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:belowc,i.e.,FB(c)−FA(c), and the slope of the CDF,fA(c), to estimate UQPE≈−FB(c)−FA(c)fA(c). The dashed (green) triangle shows the
33:1008:87710:10:0:72:414:316:6:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f6:geometry of the RIF approximation to the UQPE, with is represented by the length of the triangle’s base.

45:1242:110538:10:9:129:490:148:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:and the last two columns further add tax credits (EITC and child tax credits) to the family income definition. Medium and long run

45:1244:110801:9:10:129:490:130:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:ous up to two- and three-year lagged log minimum wage coefficients, respectively, and dividing by the negative of the family income
45:1245:110932:10:9:129:491:120:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:density at the appropriate quantile. To calculate elasticities, the UQPE estimates are subsequently divided by the family income cut-
45:1246:111065:9:9:129:490:111:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:off for the quantile. The regression specification includes state fixed effects, division-specific year effects, state-specific indicator for
45:1247:111206:9:9:129:490:102:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:each Great Recession year, state-specific linear trends, and state-level covariates (GDP per capita, EITC supplement, unemployment
45:1248:111336:9:10:129:491:93:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:rate), and individual demographic controls (quartic in age, as well as dummies for race, marital status, family size, number of chil-
45:1249:111469:10:9:129:490:83:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:dren, education level, Hispanic status, and gender), and are weighted by March CPS person weights. State-cluster-robust standard
45:1250:111597:9:9:129:187:74:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:errors in parentheses.

47:1281:116162:9:9:115:505:252:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:share with family income under various multiples of the federal poverty threshold. These estimates are from linear probability models that
47:1282:116300:9:10:115:504:243:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:regress an indicator for having family income below multiples (between 0.50 and 1.75) of the federal poverty threshold on distributed lags of
47:1283:116441:10:9:115:504:233:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:log minimum wage and covariates. The leading elasticities are calculated by dividing the one-year leading minimum wage coefficients by the
47:1284:116579:9:9:115:504:224:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:sample proportion under the family income cutoff. The long-run elasticities are calculated by dividing the sum of the contemporaneous up to
47:1285:116718:9:10:115:504:215:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:three-year lagged log minimum wage coefficients by the sample proportion under the family income cutoff. All specifications include state and
47:1286:116859:10:9:115:506:205:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:year fixed effects, state-level covariates (GDP per capita, EITC supplement, unemployment rate), and individual demographic controls (quar-
47:1287:116998:9:9:115:504:196:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:tic in age, as well as dummies for race, marital status, family size, number of children, education level, Hispanic status, and gender), and are
47:1288:117142:9:10:115:505:187:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:weighted by March CPS person weights.  Additional division-by-year fixed effects, state specific linear trends and state specific indicators for
47:1289:117286:10:9:115:403:177:4:g_d0_f6:4:g_d0_f6:each Great Recession year are indicated in the table. State-cluster-robust standard errors in parentheses.

54:1400:127617:0:29:72:540:709:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:Online Appendix A  Assessing the existing research on minimum
54:1401:127678:29:33:71:467:680:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:wages, family incomes and poverty (not for publication)
54:1402:127733:33:22:72:540:647:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In this online appendix, I review the key papers on the topic of minimum wages and family income
54:1403:127829:22:22:72:540:625:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:distribution based on U.S. data, and discuss their findings and limitations. My primary goal here is
54:1404:127929:22:22:72:540:603:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to provide a quantitative summary of the existing evidence, focusing on the poverty rate elasticity as
54:1405:128031:22:22:72:540:581:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the most commonly estimated distributional statistic. I begin by describing the process of selecting
54:1406:128131:22:22:72:542:559:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:studies for this review. First, I only consider peer-reviewed publications since the early 1990s, i.e.,
54:1407:128234:22:22:72:540:537:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the beginning of the “new economics of the minimum wage” literature. Second, I only include
54:1408:128325:22:22:72:542:515:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:studies that report estimates for some statistic based on family incomes (such as poverty, quantiles,
54:1409:128426:22:22:72:539:493:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:etc), and not other outcomes such as utilization of public assistance.27I review one additional
54:1410:128521:22:22:72:540:471:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:paper (Neumark and Wascher 2002) that I do not include in my quantitative summary. As I explain
54:1411:128616:22:21:72:540:449:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:below, their estimates on gross flows in and out of poverty do not have a clear implication for net
54:1412:128715:21:22:72:540:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:changes in poverty. Third, studies are included only when they empirically estimate the effect of
54:1413:128812:22:22:72:540:406:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:minimum wages, as opposed to simulate such effects. This selection process yields 14 studies, 13 of
54:1414:128911:22:22:71:539:384:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which are used in my quantitative summary. I note that a recent book by Belman and Wolfson
54:1415:129001:22:22:70:539:362:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(2014) provides a review of many of the same papers. Finally, I note that seven of these 14 papers
54:1416:129099:22:22:71:541:340:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:were also reviewed by Neumark and Wascher in their 2008 book,Minimum Wages; Dube (2011)
54:1417:129186:22:22:72:312:318:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discusses some of the shortcomings of that review.
54:1418:129236:22:22:88:539:296:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:As a way to quantify the existing evidence, Table A.1 reports the key estimates from the 13
54:1419:129327:22:22:72:540:274:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:studies for which I could construct an elasticity of the poverty rate with respect to the minimum
54:1420:129424:22:22:71:539:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:wage. When the original estimates are not reported as poverty rate elasticities, I use information in
54:1421:129525:22:22:72:539:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the paper to convert them (and standard errors) to that format for comparability.28To minimize
54:1422:129619:22:22:72:541:208:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the impact of subjective judgment, I have used the following guidelines for selecting estimates. (1.)
54:1423:129720:22:23:72:540:186:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:I report estimates for all of the demographic groups studied in each paper; the sole exception is
54:1424:129817:23:15:83:540:163:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:27I do not include Page et al. (2005) in my quantitative summary as they do not consider the impact on family
54:1425:129926:15:11:72:540:148:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:incomes generally, but rather only on welfare caseload. However, I note that this study stands out methodologically in
54:1426:130044:11:11:72:540:137:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:using a wide array of specifications, some of which are similar to the ones used in this paper, such as state-specific
54:1427:130162:11:11:72:540:126:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:trends and state-specific business cycle controls. The authors tend to find a positive impact of minimum wages on
54:1428:130275:11:11:71:541:115:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:welfare caseload, which appears to go against the tenor of my findings, especially the reductions in non-cash transfers.
54:1429:130395:11:11:72:540:104:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:However, as they point out, their estimates seem to vary based on the sample period. Moreover, since cash income
54:1430:130507:11:11:72:540:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:used in this paper (and in official poverty estimates) include public assistance, it is possible for both poverty to fall
54:1431:130628:11:7:72:185:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:and welfare caseload to rise.
54:1432:130657:7:0:83:539:75:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:28For simplicity, I convert the standard errors to elasticities using the same conversion factor as the point estimate.
55:1433:130778:0:22:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for workers, since minimum wages can affect who is in that group and lead to sample selection
55:1434:130871:22:22:72:540:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:problems. (2.) When a study uses multiple econometric specifications, I include all of them in
55:1435:130965:22:22:71:540:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Table A.1, except: (a.) the handful of estimates that did not include state and time fixed effects (or
55:1436:131067:22:22:72:542:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:equivalent) as controls; (b.) estimates from sub-periods reported in Addison and Blackburn (1999).
55:1437:131165:22:22:72:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Overall, these guidelines lead me to report 78 elasticities in Table A.1, which represent either all or
55:1438:131268:22:22:72:540:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:nearly all of the estimates of minimum wage impact on the poverty rate available in each of the
55:1439:131363:22:22:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:papers. Finally, besides the poverty rate, the table also reports estimates for some of the other
55:1440:131460:22:22:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:distributional statistics that are reported in the papers, including elasticities for proportions earning
55:1441:131565:22:22:72:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:below cutoffs other than the official poverty line, family earnings quantiles, and the squared poverty
55:1442:131667:22:22:72:92:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:gap.
55:1443:131671:22:22:88:539:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In my discussion below, I mostly use a chronological order, except for the three papers by
55:1444:131761:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Neumark and Wascher which I discuss together at the end. After reviewing the individual papers, I
55:1445:131858:22:21:72:540:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:provide summaries of the poverty rate elasticities in the literature. I also discuss and compare the
55:1446:131958:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:individual estimates for specific demographic groups when I present results from my own subgroup
55:1447:132054:22:22:72:179:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:analysis in section 3.1.
55:1448:132078:22:22:88:539:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Card and Krueger (1995) consider the short run impact of the 1990 federal minimum wage
55:1449:132164:22:22:71:539:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increase on the poverty rate for those 16 years or older, and regress the change in the state-level
55:1450:132263:22:22:72:540:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:poverty rate between 1989 and 1991 on the the proportion earning below the new federal wage in
55:1451:132357:22:22:71:539:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1989 (“fraction affected”). While they do not report minimum wage elasticitiesper se(reporting
55:1452:132451:22:22:72:540:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:instead the coefficient on “fraction affected”), I calculate the implicit elasticities for the poverty
55:1453:132553:22:22:72:539:270:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:rate and family earnings percentiles with respect to the minimum wage for ease of comparability.29
55:1454:132651:22:22:71:541:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Their bivariate specification has an implied minimum wage elasticity for the poverty rate of -0.39,
55:1455:132750:22:22:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but controls for employment and regional trends reduce the overall elasticity in magnitude to the
55:1456:132847:22:22:72:542:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:range (-0.36, -0.08), and the estimates are not statistically significant at the conventional levels.
55:1457:132948:22:22:71:539:182:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:They also find that the 10th percentile of the (unadjusted) family earnings distribution responds
55:1458:133045:22:30:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:positively to the minimum wage increase, with an implied elasticity between 0.28 (bivariate) and
55:1459:133141:30:15:83:540:130:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:29The mean of “fraction affected” is 0.074, the minimum wage increased by 26.9% in 1990, and the average poverty
55:1460:133253:15:11:72:540:115:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:rate in their sample is reported to be 10.6% during 1989-1991. Starting with a coefficient of -0.15 from a regression of
55:1461:133373:11:11:70:539:104:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:“fraction affected” on the proportion under poverty, I multiply this coefficient by a conversion factor of0.0740.269to obtain
55:1462:133498:11:11:72:540:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:a minimum wage semi-elasticity for the proportion under poverty, and then I further divide by 0.135 to obtain the
55:1463:133611:11:10:72:539:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:minimum wage elasticity for the proportion under poverty:−0.15×0.0740.269×10.106=−0.39.I use the same conversion
55:1464:133723:10:0:72:489:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:factor to obtain the standard errors, and perform analogous conversions for family earnings percentiles.
56:1465:133829:0:22:72:539:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:0.20 (with controls); these are statistically significant at conventional levels.30A major limitation
56:1466:133930:22:22:72:540:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of this analysis is that the estimates are imprecise. This is mainly due to the very short panel
56:1467:134026:22:22:72:540:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:structure. For example, the 95 percent confidence interval associated with the poverty rate elasticity
56:1468:134128:22:22:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in their most saturated model is quite wide: (-0.65, 0.49). Other limitations include the use of the
56:1469:134228:22:22:70:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:“fraction affected” measure of the treatment: it is possible that there were different latent trends
56:1470:134328:22:22:72:540:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in poverty across low- and high-wage states. Subsequent work has mostly used as the treatment
56:1471:134421:22:22:72:499:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:measure the log of the effective minimum wage (originally suggested in Card et al. 1994).
56:1472:134510:22:22:88:539:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Addison and Blackburn (1999) consider teens, young adults, and junior high dropouts between
56:1473:134601:22:22:71:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1983-1996. Using state-year aggregated data and two-way fixed effects, they find sizable poverty
56:1474:134697:22:22:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rate elasticities for teens and junior high dropouts in the range of (-0.61, -0.17), with an average of
56:1475:134800:22:22:70:540:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:-0.43. They find more modest sized estimates for young adults (an average elasticity of -0.21). Their
56:1476:134901:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimates for teens and junior high dropouts are often statistically significant, but the estimates are
56:1477:135004:22:21:72:539:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:likely less precise than reported since they do not account for serial correlation.31Additionally, their
56:1478:135108:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:teen results are somewhat sensitive to the inclusion of state trends, as shown in Table A.1. Morgan
56:1479:135207:22:22:72:540:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Kickham (2001) study child poverty using a two-way fixed effects model with data between
56:1480:135299:22:22:71:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1987 and 1996, and find a poverty rate elasticity of -0.39. Their estimate is statistically significant
56:1481:135402:22:22:72:540:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:using panel-corrected standard errors (which however may be inadequate). Stevans and Sessions
56:1482:135495:22:22:70:539:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(2001) consider the overall poverty rate in the 1984-1998 period; their most comparable estimate is
56:1483:135594:22:22:72:540:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from a two-way fixed effects model, and appears to yield an elasticity of -0.28.32Gundersen and
56:1484:135689:22:22:72:540:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Ziliak (2004) consider the impact of a variety of social policies on the poverty rate and the squared
56:1485:135790:22:22:72:540:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:poverty gap using both post and pre-tax income data between 1981 and 2000. For the population
56:1486:135883:22:22:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:overall, they find a small overall poverty rate elasticity of -0.03, with a range of -0.02 to -0.06 across
56:1487:135989:22:22:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:demographic groups. However, they specifically control for the wage distribution, including the
56:1488:136084:22:22:72:542:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ratio of 80th-to-20th percentile wages. This inclusion of the inequality measures is problematic,
56:1489:136181:22:19:72:542:182:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as it could block the key channel through which minimum wages would actually reduce poverty,
56:1490:136273:19:15:83:539:163:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:30Because they are using state-aggregated data from only two periods, these results are not subject to the criticism
56:1491:136389:15:11:72:540:148:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:of using standard errors that are likely understated due to intraclass or serial correlation (Bertrand et al. 2004), a
56:1492:136507:11:7:72:440:137:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:problem which does affect numerous other papers in the literature as described in the text.
56:1493:136598:7:11:83:436:130:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:31The standard errors of the long-run estimates are derived from the reported p-values.
56:1494:136685:11:15:83:540:119:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:32I say “appears” because although Stevans and Sessions say they are estimating a log-log model, their Table 2
56:1495:136795:15:11:72:540:104:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:reports a “log of poverty rate” sample mean of 14.6, a “log of minimum wage” sample mean of 3.42, and a coefficient
56:1496:136910:11:11:72:540:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:on the log minimum of -1.18. These three statistics suggest that the estimated specification was actually in levels, so
56:1497:137029:11:10:72:539:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:that the implied elasticity is likely given by−1.18×3.4214.6=−0.28. I note additionally that their standard errors also
56:1498:137148:10:0:72:219:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:do not account for serial correlation.
57:1499:137188:0:22:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:namely raising wages at the lower end of the wage distribution.33Additionally, while their estimates
57:1500:137288:22:22:72:540:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:are statistically significant, their standard errors are likely overstated since they do not account
57:1501:137388:22:22:72:540:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for serial correlation. DeFina (2008) uses state-aggregated data from 1991-2002 and finds that
57:1502:137482:22:22:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:minimum wages reduce child poverty in woman-headed families, including those headed by someone
57:1503:137576:22:22:71:541:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:without a college degree. The estimated poverty rate elasticities are -0.42 and -0.35, respectively;
57:1504:137676:22:22:71:542:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:while they are statistically significant, the standard errors also do not account for serial correlation.
57:1505:137781:22:22:88:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Burkhauser and Sabia (2007) examine the effects on state-level poverty rates for 16-64 year
57:1506:137872:22:22:72:542:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:olds and single mothers during the 1988-2003 period using specifications with two-way fixed effects.
57:1507:137972:22:22:72:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Depending on controls, their estimates of the poverty rate elasticity range between -0.08 and -0.19 for
57:1508:138075:22:22:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the population overall, and between -0.07 and -0.16 for single mothers. While none of the estimates
57:1509:138174:22:22:72:540:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:are statistically significant, the point estimates are all negative, and the confidence intervals are
57:1510:138275:22:22:72:539:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consistent with sizable effects.34In a follow-up study, Sabia and Burkhauser (2010) consider the
57:1511:138371:22:21:71:539:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2003-2007 period and income cutoffs of 100, 125, and 150 percent of the federal poverty line for the
57:1512:138471:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:population of 16-64 year olds, and find little effect. This study is limited by a rather short sample
57:1513:138572:22:22:72:540:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:period. Since it is an update of their previous paper, it is unfortunate that they do not also report
57:1514:138673:22:22:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimates using the full sample (1988-2007) instead of just considering a five year period. While
57:1515:138770:22:22:72:538:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:their point estimate is small (-0.05), the 95 percent confidence interval is fairly wide (-0.34, 0.24).
57:1516:138873:22:22:88:539:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Sabia (2008) uses individual level CPS data from 1992-2005, and a two-way fixed effects
57:1517:138960:22:22:72:542:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:specification augmented with state-specific quadratic trends to study the effect on single mothers.
57:1518:139059:22:22:72:540:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:He finds statistically insignificant but again mostly negative and often sizable estimates, with a
57:1519:139157:22:22:72:540:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:poverty rate elasticity of -0.22 from his main specification; for single mothers without a high school
57:1520:139259:22:22:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:degree, the estimate is larger in magnitude (-0.28) while still not statistically significant. Sabia
57:1521:139359:22:22:72:541:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Nielsen (2015) use the SIPP between 1996-2007 and find an overall point estimate of -0.31
57:1522:139452:22:22:70:541:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(without state-specific linear trends) or -0.03 (with trends). However, these are imprecise estimates,
57:1523:139554:22:22:72:540:182:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as the 95 percent confidence intervals are (-0.93, 0.30) and (-0.27, 0.22), respectively—the former
57:1524:139653:22:19:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:set is consistent with nearly all other estimates in the literature. The long-run point estimate is
57:1525:139752:19:15:83:539:141:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:33Another potentially problematic aspect of their methodology is the inclusion of lagged outcomes as controls along
57:1526:139867:15:11:71:539:126:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:with state fixed effects; they do state in a footnote that their results are robust to various IV strategies to account for
57:1527:139990:11:11:72:540:115:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:the bias. Furthermore, in contrast to other studies discussed here, Gundersen and Ziliak (2004) limit their sample to
57:1528:140107:11:7:72:320:104:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:families with some positive income (not necessarily earnings).
57:1529:140169:7:15:83:541:97:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:34Moreover, their estimates’ precision is likely overstated due their use of conventional (as opposed to clustered)
57:1530:140284:15:10:72:540:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:standard errors. Some of their estimates use a parametric serial correlation correction which may also be inadequate
57:1531:140400:10:0:70:234:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:(see Bertrand Duflo Mullainathan 2004).
58:1532:140441:0:22:72:539:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:negative (-0.245) with an even larger 95% confidence interval (-1.21, 0.71).35Their estimates also
58:1533:140539:22:22:72:540:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:appear to be sensitive to the inclusion of state-specific trends, but again, the imprecision of the
58:1534:140638:22:22:72:540:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimates makes it difficult to draw any firm conclusion. Overall, two of the four papers coauthored
58:1535:140738:22:22:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by Burkhauser and/or Sabia suggest small to modest negative effects, while the other two produce
58:1536:140834:22:22:72:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:fairly imprecise or fragile estimates. However, the overall evidence from their papers does not rule
58:1537:140934:22:22:72:280:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:out moderate sized poverty rate elasticities.
58:1538:140979:22:22:88:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Sabia (2014) responds to an earlier version of this paper, and reports estimates for 16-64 year
58:1539:141074:22:22:71:539:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:olds using state-year aggregated data from 1979-2012. He estimates a static two-way fixed effects
58:1540:141171:22:22:72:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:model, as well as a static model with state-specific trends, division-period effects, and state-specific
58:1541:141275:22:22:72:542:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:recession effects: these controls are similar to what I use in this paper (and in the previous version).
58:1542:141379:22:22:72:540:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:His estimates for the static two-way fixed effects model is 0.06, while from the more saturated model
58:1543:141480:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is -0.16; neither is statistically significant. His exclusion of children is one likely reason his estimates
58:1544:141588:22:21:72:540:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:are smaller in magnitude than what I find. But more importantly, the fact that the elasticity from
58:1545:141686:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the static two-way fixed effects model is small is similar to what I show in Table 7; not accounting
58:1546:141786:22:22:72:499:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for leading effects produces a positive bias, especially for the two-way fixed effects model.
58:1547:141879:22:22:88:541:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:David Neumark has authored three papers (two with coauthors) that are of particular relevance.
58:1548:141973:22:22:71:539:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Neumark and Wascher (2002) consider movements in and out of poverty by forming two-year panels
58:1549:142067:22:22:72:540:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of families with matched March CPS data between 1986 and 1995. Because they do not directly
58:1550:142158:22:22:72:542:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimate the effect of the policy on poverty rates, Table 1 does not include estimates from this paper.
58:1551:142261:22:22:71:539:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Their results seem to suggest that initially poor individuals are less likely to remain poor after a
58:1552:142361:22:22:72:540:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:minimum wage increase, while the initially non-poor are slightly more likely to enter poverty. They
58:1553:142460:22:22:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:interpret the greater churning as a negative attribute of minimum wages in creating “winners and
58:1554:142556:22:22:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:losers.” However, there are several major problems with the paper. First, the welfare implications of
58:1555:142657:22:22:72:540:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:their findings on flows are far from clear. For example, the greater churning might be a positive
58:1556:142754:22:22:72:540:182:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:attribute if it spreads both the gain and the pain more widely, and reduces the duration of poverty
58:1557:142853:22:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:spells. Second, their estimated effects on net flows into poverty (the difference between inflows and
58:1558:142954:21:22:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:outflows) are quite imprecise, and the standard errors are likely understated as they do not account
58:1559:143054:22:31:72:540:117:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for within-state correlations. They speculate that their results suggest that there was likely no effect
58:1560:143158:31:14:83:539:86:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:35Standard error of the long-run estimate is calculated by assuming the 1- and 2-year lagged minimum wages are
58:1561:143268:14:0:72:277:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:independent, as they do not report it in the paper.
59:1562:143321:0:22:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on the overall poverty rate, but this would have been easy to check using a regression where the
59:1563:143417:22:22:72:351:687:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:dependent variable is simply an indicator for being poor.36
59:1564:143476:22:22:88:539:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Neumark et al. (2005) is the only existing paper which attempts at an analysis of the impact of
59:1565:143571:22:22:72:542:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:minimum wages on the entire distribution of family incomes. Like Neumark and Wascher (2002),
59:1566:143663:22:22:72:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:they also use two-year panels of families between 1986 and 1995. They estimate the effect of discrete
59:1567:143764:22:22:72:540:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:minimum wage treatments on the distribution of the income-to-needs ratio, and their estimates
59:1568:143857:22:22:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:suggest that an increase in the minimum wage actually increases the fraction of the population in
59:1569:143954:22:22:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:poverty: they report a poverty rate elasticity of +0.39. This is the only paper in the literature that
59:1570:144056:22:22:72:542:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:I am aware of which finds such a poverty-increasing impact of the policy for the overall population,
59:1571:144156:22:22:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:so it is important to compare its methodology to other papers on the topic as well my approach
59:1572:144250:22:22:72:540:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:here. The authors are interested in estimating the counterfactual distribution of income-to-needs
59:1573:144347:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ratio for the treated state-years that experience a minimum wage increase. They implement a type
59:1574:144443:22:21:72:540:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of propensity score reweighting to adjust for demographic factors. Beyond this, however, there are
59:1575:144541:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:numerous non-standard aspects of their research design. Their method does not properly account
59:1576:144635:22:22:72:542:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for state and year fixed effects. They “mimic” state and year fixed effects by shrinking all families’
59:1577:144737:22:22:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:incomes by the proportionate change in the median income in that state (pooled over years) and also
59:1578:144836:22:22:72:539:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by analogously shrinking the median change in that year (pooled over states).37This constitutes
59:1579:144931:22:22:72:540:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an assumption that state and year effects are scale shifts that proportionately shrink the entire
59:1580:145028:22:22:72:540:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:family income distribution. In other words, they impose the assumption that various counterfactual
59:1581:145126:22:22:72:542:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:quantiles in states are moving proportionately to the median, which is an unattractive assumption,
59:1582:145224:22:22:72:540:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and much more restrictive than the inclusion of state and year dummies in a regression of the
59:1583:145317:22:22:72:539:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:poverty rate on minimum wages.38Additionally, they use anad hocadjustment in the change in
59:1584:145407:22:22:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:densities to account for the fact that some observations have both contemporaneous and lagged
59:1585:145500:22:30:72:540:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increases.39These non-standard techniques raise serious questions about the study, especially since
59:1586:145599:30:15:83:539:174:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:36In general, looking at the impact of the treatment on year-to-year inflows and outflows does not tell us what its
59:1587:145714:15:11:72:540:159:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:impact is on the stock. In the long run (i.e, reaching a new steady state) the effect of the treatment on the in- and
59:1588:145831:11:7:72:422:148:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:outflows will have to be equal by definition, even if the stock is increased or decreased.
59:1589:145921:7:15:83:539:141:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:37They also report results from a specification without any time or state fixed effects at all, and the poverty rate
59:1590:146037:15:11:72:541:126:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:elasticity from that specification was very similar. Since I screen on specifications to include (or attempt to include)
59:1591:146157:11:7:72:373:115:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:state and time fixed effects, those estimates are not reported in Table A.1.
59:1592:146233:7:15:83:540:108:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:38In this paper, my distributional analysis allows the shares under all income cutoffs to have arbitrary time-invariant
59:1593:146352:15:11:72:541:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:differences by state and years, as well as time-varying differences by census divisions, state-specific recession years,
59:1594:146472:11:7:72:172:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:and state-specific trends.
59:1595:146498:7:0:83:518:75:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:39Their statistical inference does not account for clustering of standard errors, which are likely understated.
60:1596:146611:0:22:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:it stands out in terms of producing a sizable positive poverty rate elasticity. To my knowledge, no
60:1597:146710:22:22:72:542:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:one, including any of the authors, has used this methodology in any previous or subsequent paper.
60:1598:146807:22:22:88:539:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In contrast, Neumark (2016) uses a more conventional approach to study the effect of minimum
60:1599:146899:22:22:71:539:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:wages over the 1997-2006 period; this is a follow up to an earlier paper original paper Neumark and
60:1600:146998:22:22:71:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Wascher (2011), which used a similar methodology to study minimum wage and EITC effects on
60:1601:147088:22:22:71:539:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:21-44 year olds.40He also reports these estimates for sub-groups including single women, single
60:1602:147183:22:22:71:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:women with no more than a high school degree, and single black/Hispanic women with high school
60:1603:147277:22:22:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or lesser education. Like most of the literature, he includes state and year fixed effects. He also
60:1604:147376:22:22:72:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:includes demographic and state-level controls similar to this paper, and reports estimate with and
60:1605:147474:22:22:71:209:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:without state-specific trends.
60:1606:147504:22:22:88:539:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:For the original group studied in Neumark and Wascher (2011) considered—21-44 year old family
60:1607:147597:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:heads or individuals—his results suggest a poverty rate elasticity of -0.22 without state-trends, and
60:1608:147698:22:21:70:539:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:-0.35 with trends. For a group constituting the majority of non-elderly adults, the evidence from
60:1609:147795:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Neumark (2016) suggests that minimum wages have a moderate-sized impact in reducing poverty
60:1610:147886:22:22:72:540:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and extreme poverty. When he adds older individuals (but not children), i.e., those 21 or older, the
60:1611:147986:22:22:72:542:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimates are -0.15 and -0.11, respectively. For single mothers, the estimates are -0.30 and -0.14,
60:1612:148085:22:22:72:542:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:respectively, without and with state trends. Most of the estimates are not statistically significant;
60:1613:148186:22:22:72:542:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:however, it is important to recall that these estimates are from a relatively short 9 year period.
60:1614:148284:22:22:72:542:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Importantly, however, these results are sharply different from the findings in Neumark et al. (2005),
60:1615:148385:22:22:72:299:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and much more similar to rest of the literature.
60:1616:148433:22:22:88:539:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:To take stock, the results in this literature are varied and sometimes appear to be inconsistent
60:1617:148529:22:22:71:540:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with each other. But is it possible to filter out some of the noise and actually obtain a signal?
60:1618:148626:22:22:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure A.1 plots a histogram of all 72 elasticities. First, I note that across these 13 studies, nearly
60:1619:148729:22:22:72:540:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:all (72) of the 78 estimates of the poverty rate elasticity are negative in sign. Table A.2 reports
60:1620:148828:22:22:72:540:182:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that the simple average of elasticities across all these 78 estimates is -0.18. If we take the median
60:1621:148929:22:30:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimate from each study, the simple average of these median estimates is -0.17. Indeed, only one
60:1622:149026:30:15:83:539:130:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:40Neumark (2016) follows up on an original paper Neumark and Wascher (2011), which mostly focused on the
60:1623:149130:15:11:72:540:115:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:interaction of EITC and minimum wages on on wage and employment effects. However, in Neumark and Wascher
60:1624:149234:11:11:70:539:104:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:(2011), they do provide some evidence of minimum wage effects on the share of 21-44 year olds with incomes below the
60:1625:149350:11:11:72:540:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:poverty line and one-half the poverty line. In an earlier draft of this paper, I had backed out the implied minimum
60:1626:149465:11:10:71:540:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:wage elasticities implied in Neumark and Wascher (2011). In response, Neumark (2016) went back and directly
60:1627:149572:10:0:72:233:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:estimated and reported these estimates.
61:1628:149613:0:22:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:study by Neumark et al. (2005) suggests that minimum wages actually increase the overall poverty
61:1629:149709:22:22:72:540:687:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rate. As I discussed above, this study uses an unconventional methodology that is both different
61:1630:149805:22:22:72:424:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from all other studies, and makes problematic distributional assumptions.
61:1631:149878:22:22:88:539:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Second, if we take the average of the poverty rate elasticities for the overall population across
61:1632:149975:22:22:72:540:621:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the eight studies that provide such an estimate, we obtain an average poverty rate elasticity of
61:1633:150071:22:22:70:540:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:-0.13.41There are, of course, other ways of aggregating estimates across studies. However, when I
61:1634:150168:22:22:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consider the set of nearly all available estimates of the effect of minimum wages on poverty, the
61:1635:150265:22:22:71:540:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:weight of the evidence suggests that minimum wages tend to have a small to moderate sized impact
61:1636:150361:22:22:72:167:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in reducing poverty.
61:1637:150381:22:22:88:539:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While there is a signal in the literature that minimum wages tend to reduce poverty, it is also
61:1638:150476:22:22:72:540:489:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:true that the existing evidence is clouded by serious limitations. These include (1) inadequate
61:1639:150571:22:22:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:assessment of time-varying state-level heterogeneity, especially in light of the evidence in Allegretto
61:1640:150674:22:21:72:540:445:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:et al. (2011, 2013) and Dube et al. (2010); (2) limited sample length and/or exclusion of more
61:1641:150768:21:22:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:recent years that have experienced substantially more variation in minimum wages; (3) insufficient
61:1642:150866:22:22:72:540:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:attention to serial and intra-group correlation in forming standard errors; (4) use of questionable
61:1643:150965:22:22:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimators; and (5) frequent omission of demographic and other covariates. In this paper, I use
61:1644:151060:22:22:72:540:358:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:more and better data along with more robust forms of controls to address these limitations in the
61:1645:151157:22:228:72:160:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:existing literature.
61:1646:151177:228:15:83:541:108:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:41These eight studies are: Card and Krueger (1995), Stevans and Sessions (2001), Gundersen and Ziliak (2004),
61:1647:151286:15:11:72:541:93:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:Neumark et al. (2005), Burkhauser and Sabia (2007), Sabia and Burkhauser (2010), and Sabia and Nielsen (2015),
61:1648:151396:11:10:72:540:82:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:and Sabia (2014). In the two studies authored by Burkhauser and Sabia, the overall poverty measure excludes those
61:1649:151509:10:0:72:346:72:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:under 16 or over 64; Card and Krueger also exclude those under 16.

67:1675:158647:0:29:72:543:709:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:Online Appendix B  Additional figures and tables (not for publi-
67:1676:158711:29:33:72:121:680:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:cation)
67:1677:158718:33:22:71:472:647:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This appendix contains additional figures and tables that are referenced in the text.
67:1678:158803:22:22:88:539:625:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Figure B.1 shows the variation in effective (maximum of state or federal) minimum
67:1679:158893:22:22:71:541:603:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:wage over time between 1984 and 2013. For each year, the figure plots the federal minimum wage,
67:1680:158988:22:22:72:540:581:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as well as the 50th, 75th, and 90th percentiles of the effective minimum wage distribution. The
67:1681:159083:22:22:72:540:559:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:figure shows substantial variation in minimum wage across states over the sample period, especially
67:1682:159182:22:22:72:155:537:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:during the 2000s.
67:1683:159199:22:22:88:539:515:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Figure B.2 reports the PDF and CDF of the family income distribution for the
67:1684:159284:22:22:71:539:493:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:non-elderly population, in multiples of the FPT. This distributions are estimated using the full
67:1685:159380:22:22:72:540:471:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:sample between 1984-2013. Panel A of Figure B.3 plots the family income quantiles (in multiples
67:1686:159475:22:21:72:540:449:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of FPT) over time. The figure shows that the income quantiles at the bottom of the distribution
67:1687:159570:21:22:72:542:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have been fairly stationary over the past three decades, although they do exhibit cyclical tendencies.
67:1688:159672:22:22:72:540:406:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Panel B of Appendix Figure B.3 shows the probability densities associated with family income
67:1689:159764:22:22:72:539:384:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cutoffs (fA(c(τ)), which have also been fairly stable over time, with the possible exception of the
67:1690:159863:22:22:72:540:362:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:5th quantile. The relative stability of the income-to-needs quantiles and densities is relevant for
67:1691:159962:22:22:72:539:340:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:interpreting the UQPE estimates. The estimation of the UQPE for a particular quantile,τ, is based
67:1692:160059:22:22:72:541:318:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on changes in the proportion below the income-to-needs cutoffc(τ)associated with that quantile,
67:1693:160154:22:22:72:541:296:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f11:along with the probability density of the income-to-needs ratio at that cutoff,fA(c(τ)). Bothc(τ)
67:1694:160251:22:22:72:539:274:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:andfA(y)are calculated by averaging over the entire sample. The relative stability of the mapping
67:1695:160348:22:22:72:539:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:betweencandτover this period suggests that the estimated impact on income around a given
67:1696:160436:22:22:72:403:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cutoffcis referring to roughly the same quantile over this full period.
